## The Academic Rivalry (í•™ë¬¸ì  ê²½ìŸ)

---

**EN:** Marilla **pessimistically expected more trouble** since Anne had again begun to go to school. But **none developed**. Perhaps Anne caught something of the â€œmodelâ€ spirit from Minnie Andrews; at least she got on very well with Mr. Phillips thenceforth. She flung herself into her studies **heart and soul**, determined **not to be outdone in any class by Gilbert Blythe**. The **rivalry** between them was soon apparent; it was **entirely good natured on Gilbertâ€™s side**; but it is much to be feared that the same thing cannot be said of Anne, who had certainly an **unpraiseworthy tenacity for holding grudges**. She was **as intense in her hatreds as in her loves**. She would **not stoop to admit that she meant to rival Gilbert** in schoolwork, because that would have been to **acknowledge his existence** which Anne **persistently ignored**; but the rivalry was there and **honors fluctuated between them**. Now Gilbert was head of the spelling class; now Anne, with a **toss of her long red braids**, spelled him down. One morning Gilbert had all his sums done correctly and had his name written on the blackboard on the **roll of honor**; the next morning Anne, having wrestled **wildly with decimals** the entire evening before, would be first. One **awful day they were ties** and their names were written up together. It was **almost as bad as a take-notice** and Anneâ€™s **mortification** was as evident as Gilbertâ€™s satisfaction. When the written examinations at the end of each month were held the **suspense was terrible**. The first month Gilbert came out three marks ahead. The second Anne beat him by five. But her triumph was **marred** by the fact that **Gilbert congratulated her heartily before the whole school**. It would have been ever so much sweeter to her if he had **felt the sting of his defeat**.

## The Premier's Visit and Anne's Return to Diana

This chapter is a wonderful example of how a large political event can trigger an intimate personal triumph. Here is the full text, arranged by paragraph in English, followed by the Korean translation.

---

EN: After the Christmas concert's excitement, Anne found life "fearfully flat, stale, and unprofitable," fearing she was now "spoiled for everyday life." ğŸ‘§ Anne Turns Thirteen and Her 'Besetting Sin' (ì•¤ì˜ ì—´ì„¸ ì‚´ ìƒì¼ê³¼ 'ê³ ì§ˆì ì¸ ì£„')
EN: On her thirteenth birthday, Anne felt an "awed" sense of entering her teens, which made life "so much more interesting." She mused on growing up, which she hoped would let her use "big words" without ridicule. Discussing "besetting sins" with Mrs. Allan, whom she deeply admired, Anne confessed hers was "imagining too much and forgetting my duties," a fault she resolved to overcome now that she was thirteen. She also showed her unique moral perspective, arguing that hearing about a minister who stole a strawberry tart as a boy was "encouraging," not shocking, as it proved that people who make mistakes can still grow up to be good.

ğŸ“ The Story Club and Literary Masterpieces (ì´ì•¼ê¸° í´ëŸ½ê³¼ ë¬¸í•™ì  ê±¸ì‘)
EN: While Diana dreaded having to write a story "out of our own heads," Anne found it "as easy as wink."
